Summary of Manufacturing AI Transformation (AX) Trend Analysis Results

This document summarizes the latest technology and trend analysis results related to AI transformation (AX) in the manufacturing industry. The analysis shows that 'predictive maintenance' and 'digital twin' have emerged as the most critical technologies driving AI adoption in current manufacturing sites. Predictive maintenance is a technology that analyzes equipment data to predict and respond to failures in advance, and digital twin is a technology that implements actual equipment or processes precisely in a virtual world to find optimal operating methods through simulation.

Key trends include the emergence of 'Edge AI' technology, which processes data in real-time at production sites, and the importance of data governance and standardization for successful AI adoption is being strongly emphasized. This provides opportunities to maximize productivity through AI, while also indicating that integration with existing factory automation systems (PLC/SCADA) and building data infrastructure are important challenges.

In the future, 'Generative AI' and 'Industrial Foundation Models' are expected to combine with robotics, knowledge retrieval, and other applications, to implement more intelligent and autonomous production environments and accelerate manufacturing innovation.

Michelin CIO's discussion clarifies that the SDV transition demands digital twin, open API, and app ecosystem strategies from parts and materials manufacturers. Michelin is experimenting with monetization through a three-pronged strategy of selling tire digital twins, in-vehicle embedded software, and app store services, suggesting that a subscription/license model centered on 'functional value' is realistic for data. In the factory domain, AI has a significant impact on machine vision, energy optimization, and waste reduction, while GenAI has shown rapid results in areas like maintenance knowledge support and document summarization. Agentic AI, as automation succeeding RPA, has shown some cost savings, leading to a proposal for expansion to a 1% to 10% adoption rate. Organizationally, it is recommended to combine R&D incubation with in-house operationalization and to enhance the ability to handle high-mix, low-volume production by increasing manufacturing flexibility. Manufacturing sites can implement these practices through building digital twins/threads, standardizing OEM data contracts, experimenting with function-based pricing, prioritizing AI tasks for energy and quality, deploying Copilot/GenAI, and phased expansion of agentic AI pilots.

When introducing manufacturing AI, such as for visual inspection, first check data and environmental requirements, including illumination, glare, camera angle, environmental stabilization, securing representative data with normal/defective balance and boundary cases, and clarifying sensor cycles, timestamps, and fault labels for predictive maintenance. Under the "do not break anything" principle, apply shadow mode, gradual rollout, and confidence threshold-based HITL, and design field deployment and scale with standard schema, governance, MLOps (version control/monitoring/retraining), and an edge-cloud architecture. Initially, select high-ROI tasks such as quality inspection, scrap/rework reduction, and anomaly detection, and by tracking their effects linked to FTQ, PPM, and OEE, you can avoid the pilot-for-performance trap.

With AI 3D vision that recognizes transparent, reflective, and black parts with sub-millimeter precision within 0.2 seconds, regardless of lighting changes, it resolves the blind spots of existing automation and simultaneously improves UPH and FTQ in high-speed picking and precision assembly. In semiconductor manufacturing, by transitioning from rule-based and pixel counter methods to AI vision, false positives/missed detections are reduced and throughput/FPY are increased, while performance can be maintained without line downtime through continuous learning and automatic recalibration, even with changes in lighting and product mix. By integrating inspection images, equipment sensor data, and maintenance logs, predictive maintenance is enhanced, and root cause analysis and parameter optimization are accelerated through interactive analysis to build operational intelligence. From an execution perspective, simultaneously implement MLOps/model version control, edge GPU deployment, human-in-the-loop, domain shift validation, and ensure traceability; and expedite recipe validation and change approval through digital twin simulations.

The core of the article is data-driven design principles for scaling AI. The message is to establish factory data as a single source of truth using an Unified Namespace (UNS), and to consistently operate integration, governance, and MLOps with enterprise systems within an AI data cloud. This enables enterprise-wide AI deployment, end-to-end visibility, secure data collaboration with partners, and resilience. Practically, establishing common data model/namespace rules, building real-time pipelines and data catalogs, formalizing security and sharing policies along with MLOps, and a phased rollout starting with high-ROI AI use cases are effective.

This A.S. curriculum is suitable as a benchmark template for smart manufacturing workforce development, presenting a practical roadmap that progresses from Mathematics/Physics â†’ Electronics/Mechatronics â†’ Industrial Control (PLC) â†’ Robotics/Programming â†’ AI/ML â†’ Capstone. In 2025, manufacturing AR will move beyond pilot projects, proving productivity improvements in areas like training, assembly overlays, and logistics picking, accelerating ROI-centric adoption. Based on the success of a single process, progressively expand multi-process and multi-factory rollouts, but include operational requirements such as PPE compatibility, battery swappability, and MDM/security in the device selection criteria.

End-to-end automation examples of purpose-built AI agents (e.g., debt collection, regulatory compliance, payroll approval, invoice payment) validated in fintechâ€”achieving up to approximately 40% performance improvementâ€”are directly applicable to manufacturing finance and back-office AX. Manufacturers should pilot AP/AR, O2C collection, trade compliance, and supplier payment approvals; implement deep integration with ERP (SAP/Oracle), policy/authorization control, human-in-the-loop (HITL) review, and log/audit trails; and manage DSO/DPO, touchless ratio, exception rate, and collection rate as KPIs. Applying multi-objective optimization inspired by slime mold to factory layout, material flow, AMR/AGV routes, and piping and power line design allows for simultaneously exploring trade-offs between minimal network, resilience, and CAPEX. The approach of designing urban infrastructure by mimicking the resource allocation strategy of slime molds offers a clue for multi-objective optimization of layout and logistics networks in manufacturing as well. Apple's Houston AI server production demonstrates the acceleration of reshoring and the formation of a regional hub-type ecosystem, through which supply chain integrationâ€”from OEM/ODM to racks/power, high-speed interconnects, and coolingâ€”can simultaneously lower lead time, security, and geopolitical risks. Manufacturers must strengthen core capabilities such as high-density power/thermal design, liquid cooling, reliability testing, and security/traceability, and secure a talent pipeline by linking with local technical training programs. While this is a list of research topics for doctoral programs by department, it broadly presents trends directly relevant to Manufacturing AX. Digital Twin, AI-based machining and process control, predictive maintenance, edge/federated learning, 5G/6G, SHM sensors, and DfAM are topics for which PoC can be designed immediately. For manufacturers, it is effective to prepare for regulatory and trust issues by establishing data governance, MLOps, and on-device acceleration infrastructure, internalizing XAI and security, in conjunction with joint research with academia and the formation of talent pipelines. Topics related to energy, power electronics, and CCUS are worth considering as strategic investment priorities from the perspective of simultaneously achieving factory energy efficiency and carbon reduction KPIs.

HBM4 memory (2x bandwidth, 40%+ power efficiency) improves throughput and latency for memory-bound/latency-sensitive workloads such as vision inspection, predictive maintenance, and digital twin, thereby simultaneously boosting the performance, TCO, and ESG metrics of smart factories. With SK hynix's HBM4 mass production, AI training/inference performance and power efficiency are significantly improved, which can simultaneously lower throughput, latency, and energy costs for manufacturing AI workloads such as vision inspection and digital twin. While ETF performance and the government's 30 trillion won investment in robots have propelled humanoids and quantum computing as promising pillars for manufacturing automation, do not mistake ETF returns for maturity; instead, independently evaluate them based on TRL, pilot data, and TCO/ROI. For humanoids, prioritize PoC in low-load, semi-structured tasks such as internal logistics transfer, simple assembly, and EoL handling. HRC safety, warehouse/MHE integration, and standard work design are crucial for success. To mitigate lead time risks for core components (actuators, harmonic drives, batteries), consider dual-sourcing and long-term contracts. For quantum computing, establish a 12-18 month roadmap for scheduling, routing, and material optimization using quantum-inspired algorithms/simulators and QCaaS partnerships, and actively leverage government subsidies, tax incentives, and demonstration projects.

In the analysis of new ETFs, humanoid robots and quantum computing recorded high returns, and the Korean government also announced large-scale investment in the robotics industry. This is a sign that the commercialization of future automation and optimization technologies and the speed of supply chain formation will accelerate in manufacturing. In the short term, humanoid PoC (e.g., internal logistics, simple assembly), establishment of safety and process interlocking systems, and preparation for dual-sourcing key components are advisable. For quantum computing, pursuing scheduling and routing PoC using quantum-inspired algorithms and actively utilizing government support and demonstration projects to distribute costs and risks is desirable. However, since market returns and technological readiness are separate, a cool-headed evaluation based on TRL and ROI is necessary.

HFS Research proposes IT-OT-ET convergence and physical AI (robotics + AI) as core transformation axes in manufacturing, and recommends a roadmap for expanding from pilot projects by verifying priority use cases such as predictive maintenance, visual inspection, process optimization, and autonomous logistics with OEE, FPY, cycle time, and energy KPIs. í†µí•© ì¸ì‚¬ì´íŠ¸:Consolidated Insight:
íƒ„ì†Œì œê±°ëŠ” ìë°œì  ìˆ˜ìš”ë§Œìœ¼ë¡œëŠ” í•œê³„ê°€ ì»¤ í–¥í›„ ì •ë¶€ ì¡°ë‹¬Â·ê·œì œê°€ ìˆ˜ìš”ë¥¼ ì¢Œìš°í•  ê°€ëŠ¥ì„±ì´ ë†’ìœ¼ë¯€ë¡œ, ì œì¡°ì—…ì€ â€˜ê°ì¶• ìš°ì„ +ì”ì—¬ë°°ì¶œ ì œê±°â€™ í¬íŠ¸í´ë¦¬ì˜¤ì™€ ì¤‘ì¥ê¸° íƒ„ì†Œë¹„ìš©Â·ì •ì±… ì‹œë‚˜ë¦¬ì˜¤ë¥¼ ê²½ì˜Â·ì¡°ë‹¬ ì „ëµì— ë°˜ì˜í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì‹¤ë¬´ì ìœ¼ë¡œëŠ” ê³µì •Â·ê³µê¸‰ë§ ë°ì´í„° ê¸°ë°˜ì˜ MRV/LCA ìë™í™”, ì œê±° í¬ë ˆë”§ ë²¤ë” ë“€ë”œë¦¬ì „ìŠ¤(í‘œì¤€Â·ê²€ì¦Â·ì§€ì—­ì‚¬íšŒ ì˜í–¥ í¬í•¨), ì¥ê¸° ì˜¤í”„í…Œì´í¬ì™€ í¬íŠ¸í´ë¦¬ì˜¤ ë‹¤ë³€í™”ë¡œ ë‹¨ê°€Â·ê³µê¸‰ ë¦¬ìŠ¤í¬ë¥¼ ê´€ë¦¬í•˜ì‹­ì‹œì˜¤. ë˜í•œ DACÂ·ê´‘ë¬¼í™” ë“± ì¸í”„ë¼ í™•ëŒ€ë¡œ ì••ì¶•ê¸°Â·ì—´êµí™˜ê¸°Â·í¡ì°©ì œ ë“± ë¶€í’ˆ ìˆ˜ìš”ê°€ ì˜ˆìƒë˜ë¯€ë¡œ ì„¤ê³„ í‘œì¤€í™”Â·ëª¨ë“ˆí™”Â·ì¡°ë¦½ ìë™í™” ì—­ëŸ‰ì„ ì„ ì œ êµ¬ì¶•í•´ ì›ê°€ê³¡ì„  í•˜ë½ê¸°ì— ëŒ€ë¹„í•˜ì‹­ì‹œì˜¤. As carbon removal is greatly limited by voluntary demand alone, and government procurement and regulations are highly likely to determine future demand, the manufacturing industry must incorporate a 'reduction first + residual emission removal' portfolio and mid-to-long term carbon cost and policy scenarios into its management and procurement strategies. Practically, manage unit cost and supply risks through automation of MRV/LCA based on process and supply chain data, due diligence of removal credit vendors (including standards, verification, and community impact), and long-term off-take agreements and portfolio diversification. Additionally, as infrastructure expansion for DAC (Direct Air Capture), mineralization, etc., is expected to increase demand for components such as compressors, heat exchangers, and adsorbents, proactively build capabilities in design standardization, modularization, and assembly automation to prepare for a period of declining cost curves.

The core point of the article is that carbon removal, due to its public good nature, has limitations with voluntary markets alone, and that government procurement, subsidies, and regulations will determine its growth. It also points out that to avoid repeating the failures of the offset market, strong standards, MRV reliability, and minimizing community impact are essential. From a manufacturing perspective, in preparation for the potential emergence of removal demand under future regulations, it is practical to pre-examine carbon removal procurement options in addition to reduction-focused strategies, and to establish MRV/LCA automation and supplier verification systems. At the same time, as infrastructure like DAC and mineralization expands, manufacturing opportunities for equipment and materials will arise, so preparing for cost competitiveness through modularization and standardization is effective.

Amid US-China tensions, a US-Southeast Asia trade agreement is expected to lead to preferential treatment for US industrial and agricultural products in Malaysia and Vietnam, and tariff elimination on US industrial goods in Cambodia and Thailand. When transitioning to a China+1 strategy, production and sourcing reallocation reflecting country-specific tariffs and preferential treatments is effective. As compliance burdens increase due to cooperation on investment screening and export controls and enhanced prevention of transshipment, implement trade compliance automation, including HS code and BOM-based origin traceability, ROO compliance, and AI-based sanctions and export control screening, in conjunction with integrated customs, logistics, and risk network simulation (digital twin). Additionally, through EDI/API standardization with local factories and partners, you can improve customs clearance and delivery time predictions to lower TCO.

With the US-Southeast Asia multilateral trade agreement, tariffs on some countries to the US will be maintained/abolished, non-tariff barriers will be lowered, and cooperation in investment screening and export controls will be strengthened. Manufacturers, under the China+1 strategy, must proceed with supply chain redesign that reflects country-specific tariffs and preferential measures, and enhance traceability/compliance automation in preparation for compliance with rules of origin and prevention of transshipment. The non-introduction of digital taxes in Malaysia and Thailand is advantageous for the adoption of cloud-based operating systems; therefore, securing digital twin/simulation capabilities, including customs clearance/delivery date prediction and trade scenario analysis, can lead to tangible results.
